## GHSA-6x6h-qqr7-855w · lightrag-hku

2026-07-20 · Python · **CRITICAL** 9.3 · Pattern: `CORS_MISCONFIGURATION→ORIGIN`

### Root cause

The application's CORS middleware was configured to allow both wildcard origins ('*') and credentialed requests (allow_credentials=True) simultaneously. This combination is explicitly disallowed by the Fetch specification and can lead to a security bypass in some browser implementations, allowing an attacker to make credentialed cross-origin requests from any origin.

### Impact

An attacker could potentially make credentialed cross-origin requests to the LightRAG API from any malicious website, bypassing the intended same-origin policy for credentialed requests. This could lead to unauthorized access or actions if the API relies on credentials (e.g., Authorization headers) for sensitive operations.

### Key diff

```
--- a/lightrag/api/lightrag_server.py
+++ b/lightrag/api/lightrag_server.py
@@ -1376,8 +1376,9 @@ def get_cors_origins():
     # Add CORS middleware
+    cors_origins = get_cors_origins()
+    allow_credentials = cors_origins != ["*"]
     app.add_middleware(
         CORSMiddleware,
-        allow_origins=get_cors_origins(),
-        allow_credentials=True,
+        allow_origins=cors_origins,
+        allow_credentials=allow_credentials,
         allow_methods=["*"],
```

### Fix summary

The patch modifies the CORS middleware configuration to dynamically set 'allow_credentials' based on the 'allow_origins' list. If the allowed origins include the wildcard ('*'), 'allow_credentials' is set to 'False'. If specific origins are listed, 'allow_credentials' remains 'True'. This ensures compliance with the Fetch spec and prevents the insecure combination.

## GHSA-vj59-8hwv-xxmv · astro

2026-07-20 · JavaScript · **HIGH** 8.2 · Pattern: `MISSING_AUTHZ→RESOURCE`

### Root cause

The vulnerability stemmed from a mismatch in how URL paths were canonicalized and decoded. Specifically, Astro's `validateAndDecodePathname` function previously attempted to iteratively decode multi-level encoded paths but did not enforce a strict limit on iterations or explicitly reject paths that were excessively encoded. This allowed an attacker to craft a URL with multiple layers of encoding (e.g., `%252F` for `/`) that could bypass middleware authorization checks, which might have seen a partially decoded path, while the internal routing mechanism eventually resolved to a different, protected path.

### Impact

An attacker could bypass authorization checks implemented in middleware by crafting specially encoded URLs. This could grant unauthorized access to protected routes or resources that would otherwise be restricted, leading to information disclosure, privilege escalation, or other unauthorized actions.

### Key diff

```
--- a/packages/astro/src/core/util/pathname.ts
+++ b/packages/astro/src/core/util/pathname.ts
@@ -33,7 +33,7 @@ export function validateAndDecodePathname(pathname: string): string {
 	// We cap iterations to prevent infinite loops on pathological input.
 	let iterations = 0;
-	while (decoded !== pathname && iterations < 10) {
+	while (decoded !== pathname) {
+		if (iterations >= MAX_DECODE_ITERATIONS) {
+			throw new MultiLevelEncodingError();
+		}
 		pathname = decoded;
 		try {
```

### Fix summary

The patch introduces a `MAX_DECODE_ITERATIONS` constant (set to 10) in `validateAndDecodePathname` to limit how many times a URL path is decoded. If a path is still changing after this many iterations, a `MultiLevelEncodingError` is thrown. The `FetchState` class now catches this specific error and sets an `invalidEncoding` flag, leading to the request being rejected with a `400` status before any middleware or routing logic is applied, preventing the use of excessively encoded paths.

## GHSA-xj96-63gp-2gmr · Pillow

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 8.2 · Pattern: `INTEGER_OVERFLOW→BOUNDARY`

### Root cause

The vulnerability stemmed from an integer overflow in the `ImagingExpand` function when calculating memory allocation size. A large `size` parameter, specifically for `ImageFilter.RankFilter`, could lead to `size * size * 4` exceeding the maximum integer value, causing the allocation to wrap around and become much smaller than required. This insufficient allocation was then followed by writes beyond the allocated buffer.

### Impact

An attacker could trigger a heap out-of-bounds write, potentially leading to denial of service (application crash) or arbitrary code execution by corrupting adjacent memory regions.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/PIL/ImageFilter.py
+++ b/src/PIL/ImageFilter.py
@@ -102,6 +102,9 @@ def __init__(self, size: int, rank: int) -> None:
         if size % 2 == 0:
             msg = "bad filter size"
             raise ValueError(msg)
+        if size * size * 4 > (2**31 - 1):
+            msg = "filter size too large"
+            raise ValueError(msg)
         if rank < 0 or rank >= size * size:
             msg = "bad rank value"
             raise ValueError(msg)
--- a/src/libImaging/Filter.c
+++ b/src/libImaging/Filter.c
@@ -59,6 +59,9 @@ ImagingExpand(Imaging imIn, int margin) {
     if (margin < 0) {
         return (Imaging)ImagingError_ValueError("bad kernel size");
     }
+    if (margin > INT_MAX / (margin * (int)sizeof(FLOAT32))) {
+        return (Imaging)ImagingError_ValueError("filter size too large");
+    }
 
     imOut =
         ImagingNewDirty(imIn->mode, imIn->xsize + 2 * margin, imIn->ysize + 2 * margin);
```

### Fix summary

The patch adds checks to prevent excessively large filter sizes. In `ImageFilter.py`, it ensures `size * size * 4` does not exceed `2^31 - 1`. In `Filter.c`, it adds a more robust check to prevent `margin * margin * sizeof(FLOAT32)` from overflowing during memory allocation calculations, thereby preventing the undersized buffer allocation.

## GHSA-7rc3-g7h6-22m7 · github.com/filebrowser/filebrowser/v2

2026-07-20 · Go · **HIGH** 8.1 · Pattern: `PRIVILEGE_ESCALATION→ROLE`

### Root cause

The application normalized usernames to create home directories. Due to a many-to-one mapping in the normalization process (cleanUsername function), distinct usernames could result in the same normalized home directory path. When a new user signed up, if their normalized username collided with an existing user's normalized username, the system would silently assign the new user the existing user's home directory without checking for conflicts.

### Impact

An attacker could create an account with a username that normalizes to the same home directory as an existing user, thereby gaining unauthorized access to the existing user's files and data. This constitutes a privilege escalation and data breach.

### Key diff

```
--- a/http/auth.go
+++ b/http/auth.go
@@ -201,6 +201,22 @@ var signupHandler = func(w http.ResponseWriter, r *http.Request, d *data) (int,
 		return http.StatusInternalServerError, err
 	}
 	user.Scope = userHome
+
+	// When home directories are created from the username, distinct usernames
+	// can normalize to the same scope (cleanUsername is many-to-one), which would
+	// silently hand the new user another user's home directory. Reject the signup
+	// if the derived scope is already taken. When CreateUserDir is off, all
+	// signups intentionally share the configured default scope, so this check
+	// does not apply.
+	if d.settings.CreateUserDir {
+		switch _, err := d.store.Users.GetByScope(user.Scope); {
+		case err == nil:
+			return http.StatusConflict, fberrors.ErrExist
+		case !errors.Is(err, fberrors.ErrNotExist):
+			return http.StatusInternalServerError, err
+		}
+	}
 	log.Printf("new user: %s, home dir: [%s].", user.Username, userHome)
 
 	err = d.store.Users.Save(user)
```

### Fix summary

The patch introduces a check during user signup to prevent username normalization collisions. Before creating a new user's home directory, it now verifies if a user with the same derived scope (home directory path) already exists. If a collision is detected, the signup is rejected with a conflict error, preventing the new user from being assigned another user's home directory.

## GHSA-j5h6-vqc3-phqh · directus

2026-07-20 · JavaScript · **HIGH** 7.7 · Pattern: `SSRF→INTERNAL_ACCESS`

### Root cause

The existing SSRF protection mechanism in Directus did not correctly block the IP address '0.0.0.0' when configured to do so. This allowed an attacker to bypass the intended network restrictions and potentially access internal resources.

### Impact

An attacker could bypass the SSRF protection to make requests to internal network resources, potentially leading to information disclosure or further attacks within the internal network.

### Key diff

```
- 			if (blockNetwork === '0.0.0.0') {
- 				blockNetworkInterfaces = true;
- 				continue;
+ 			if (blockNetwork === '0.0.0.0') {
+ 				blockNetworkInterfaces = true;
+ 				blockList.parseSubnet('0.0.0.0/8');
+ 				blockList.parseAddress('::');
+ 				continue;
  			}
```

### Fix summary

The patch explicitly adds '0.0.0.0/8' and '::' (IPv6 unspecified address) to the blocklist when '0.0.0.0' is configured as a denied network. This ensures that requests targeting these addresses are properly blocked, preventing the SSRF bypass.

## GHSA-vgj4-345g-jcf8 · github.com/cloudreve/Cloudreve/v4

2026-07-20 · Go · **HIGH** 7.6 · Pattern: `MISSING_VERIFICATION→SIGNATURE`

### Root cause

The OAuth access token issuance logic in Cloudreve did not include the `client_id` claim in the generated JWT. This omission meant that the server-side validation of access tokens could not correctly enforce the scopes associated with a specific client, allowing an attacker to potentially use an access token with broader permissions than intended for their client.

### Impact

An attacker could bypass scope enforcement, potentially gaining access to resources or performing actions that their OAuth client was not authorized for, leading to unauthorized data access or privilege escalation.

### Key diff

```
--- a/pkg/auth/jwt.go
+++ b/pkg/auth/jwt.go
@@ -257,7 +257,8 @@ func (t *tokenAuth) Issue(ctx context.Context, args *IssueTokenArgs) (*Token, er
 			NotBefore: jwt.NewNumericDate(issueDate),
 			ExpiresAt: jwt.NewNumericDate(accessTokenExpired),
 		},
-		Scopes: args.Scopes,
+		ClientID: args.ClientID,
+		Scopes:   args.Scopes,
 	}).SignedString(t.secret)
 	if err != nil
```

### Fix summary

The patch modifies the `Issue` function in `pkg/auth/jwt.go` to include the `ClientID` from the `IssueTokenArgs` in the claims of the generated JWT access token. This ensures that the `client_id` is present in the token, allowing for proper scope validation against the client's permissions.

## GHSA-23hp-3jrh-7fpw · tar

2026-07-20 · JavaScript · **CRITICAL** 7.5 · Pattern: `DOS→RESOURCE_EXHAUSTION`

### Root cause

The `node-tar` library did not limit the decompression ratio when processing compressed tar archives. An attacker could craft a malicious archive that decompresses to an extremely large size from a small compressed input, leading to excessive memory or CPU consumption.

### Impact

An attacker could provide a specially crafted tar archive to an application using `node-tar`, causing the application to consume excessive system resources (memory, CPU) and crash or become unresponsive, leading to a denial of service.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/parse.ts
+++ b/src/parse.ts
@@ -508,13 +538,22 @@ export class Parser extends EE implements Warner {
           this[UNZIP] === undefined ? new Unzip({}) 
           : isZstd ? new ZstdDecompress({}) 
           : new BrotliDecompress({}) 
-        this[UNZIP].on('data', chunk => this[CONSUMECHUNK](chunk))
-        this[UNZIP].on('error', er => this.abort(er as Error))
+        this[UNZIP].on('data', chunk => {
+          if (this[CHECKDECOMPRESSIONRATIO](chunk)) {
+            this[CONSUMECHUNK](chunk)
+          }
+        })
+        this[UNZIP].on('error', er => {
+          if (!this[ABORTED]) {
+            this.abort(er as Error)
+          }
+        })
         this[UNZIP].on('end', () => {
           this[ENDED] = true
           this[CONSUMECHUNK]()
         })
         this[WRITING] = true
+        this[COMPRESSEDBYTESREAD] += chunk.length
         const ret = !!this[UNZIP][ended ? 'end' : 'write'](chunk)
         this[WRITING] = false
         cb?.()
```

### Fix summary

The patch introduces a `maxDecompressionRatio` option, defaulting to 1000, which limits the ratio of decompressed bytes to compressed bytes. If this ratio is exceeded during decompression, the parsing process is aborted, preventing resource exhaustion.

## GHSA-45hq-cxwh-f6vc · pillow

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `DOS→RESOURCE_EXHAUSTION`

### Root cause

Pillow's font loading mechanisms (BdfFontFile, PcfFontFile, and FontFile) did not perform decompression bomb checks when creating new Image objects for glyphs or bitmaps. This allowed an attacker to craft malicious font files that, when processed, would attempt to allocate extremely large images, bypassing the intended bomb protection.

### Impact

An attacker could supply a specially crafted font file to an application using Pillow, causing it to attempt to allocate an excessively large amount of memory. This would lead to a denial of service by exhausting system resources or crashing the application.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/PIL/FontFile.py
+++ b/src/PIL/FontFile.py
@@ -89,6 +89,7 @@ def compile(self) -> None:
         self.ysize = h
 
         # paste glyphs into bitmap
+        Image._decompression_bomb_check((xsize, ysize))
         self.bitmap = Image.new("1", (xsize, ysize))
         self.metrics: list[
             tuple[tuple[int, int], tuple[int, int, int, int], tuple[int, int, int, int]]
```

### Fix summary

The patch adds calls to `Image._decompression_bomb_check()` in the `BdfFontFile`, `PcfFontFile`, and `FontFile` modules before creating new `Image` objects. This ensures that the dimensions of images derived from font data are validated against the decompression bomb limits, preventing the allocation of excessively large images.

## GHSA-52cp-r559-cp3m · js-yaml

2026-07-20 · JavaScript · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `DOS→RESOURCE_EXHAUSTION`

### Root cause

The js-yaml parser did not limit the number of merge keys processed when parsing YAML documents. Specially crafted YAML input with deeply nested or numerous merge keys could cause the parser to enter a state of quadratic CPU consumption due to repeated object merging operations.

### Impact

An attacker could provide a malicious YAML file to an application using js-yaml, leading to excessive CPU usage and potentially a denial of service for the application or the server it runs on.

### Key diff

```
--- a/lib/js-yaml/loader.js
+++ b/lib/js-yaml/loader.js
@@ -293,6 +295,10 @@ function mergeMappings(state, destination, source, overridableKeys) {
   for (index = 0, quantity = sourceKeys.length; index < quantity; index += 1) {
     key = sourceKeys[index];
 
+    if (state.maxTotalMergeKeys !== -1 && ++state.totalMergeKeys > state.maxTotalMergeKeys) {
+      throwError(state, 'merge keys exceeded maxTotalMergeKeys (' + state.maxTotalMergeKeys + ')');
+    }
+
     if (!_hasOwnProperty.call(destination, key)) {
       setProperty(destination, key, source[key]);
       overridableKeys[key] = true;
```

### Fix summary

The patch introduces a new option `maxTotalMergeKeys` to limit the total number of merge keys processed during parsing, defaulting to 10,000. A counter `totalMergeKeys` is added and incremented for each merge key. If the count exceeds the maximum, an error is thrown, preventing excessive resource consumption.

## GHSA-6r8x-57c9-28j4 · Pillow

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `INTEGER_OVERFLOW→BOUNDARY`

### Root cause

The vulnerability stemmed from using signed 32-bit integers (int) for coordinate calculations (dx0, dy0, dx1, dy1) in image processing functions like ImagingPaste and ImagingFill2. When an attacker provided sufficiently large negative coordinates, the subtraction `dx1 - dx0` or `dy1 - dy0` could result in a signed integer overflow, leading to a small positive value for `xsize` or `ysize`. This incorrect size calculation was then used to allocate or access memory, causing a heap out-of-bounds write.

### Impact

An attacker could craft malicious image coordinates to trigger a heap out-of-bounds write. This could lead to denial of service (application crash), information disclosure, or potentially arbitrary code execution by corrupting adjacent heap metadata or application data.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/libImaging/Paste.c
+++ b/src/libImaging/Paste.c
@@ -274,7 +274,7 @@ int
 ImagingPaste(
     Imaging imOut, Imaging imIn, Imaging imMask, int dx0, int dy0, int dx1, int dy1
 ) {
-    int xsize, ysize;
+    int64_t xsize, ysize;
     int pixelsize;
     int sx0, sy0;
     ImagingSectionCookie cookie;
@@ -286,8 +286,8 @@ ImagingPaste(
 
     pixelsize = imOut->pixelsize;
 
-    xsize = dx1 - dx0;
-    ysize = dy1 - dy0;
+    xsize = (int64_t)dx1 - dx0;
+    ysize = (int64_t)dy1 - dy0;
```

### Fix summary

The patch addresses the vulnerability by changing the data type for `xsize` and `ysize` from `int` to `int64_t` in the affected functions. Additionally, the coordinate differences `dx1 - dx0` and `dy1 - dy0` are explicitly cast to `int64_t` before calculation to prevent signed integer overflow, ensuring that the calculated sizes are correct even with large negative input coordinates.

> → GHSA-5r97-79vw-qvm4

> → GHSA-78vr-q6cf-c7p6

> → GHSA-8wr5-jm2h-8r4f

## GHSA-8x88-c5mf-7j5w · tar

2026-07-20 · JavaScript · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `DOS→INFINITE_LOOP`

### Root cause

The `node-tar` library did not properly validate the 'size' field when parsing tar archive headers, including those from PAX extended headers. If a negative size was provided, the internal logic for processing archive entries could enter an infinite loop when attempting to replace or process the entry.

### Impact

An attacker could craft a malicious tar archive with a negative entry size, causing a denial of service in applications that process such archives, leading to resource exhaustion and unresponsiveness.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/header.ts
+++ b/src/header.ts
@@ -103,10 +106,12 @@ export class Header implements HeaderData {
     this.gid =
       exForFields?.gid ?? gexForFields?.gid ?? decNumber(buf, off + 116, 8)
-    this.size =
+    this.size = notNegative(
       exForFields?.size ??
-      gexForFields?.size ??
-      decNumber(buf, off + 124, 12)
+        gexForFields?.size ??
+        decNumber(buf, off + 124, 12),
+    )
```

### Fix summary

The patch introduces a `notNegative` helper function to explicitly check and discard negative size values when parsing tar headers. Additionally, it modifies the PAX header parsing logic to only accept non-negative values for the 'size' field, preventing the infinite loop condition.

> ⚡ **Pattern match — 8 occurrences**
> Languages: JavaScript, Python, Java, Erlang, RUST
> → GHSA-4rc3-7j7w-m548
> → GHSA-grgv-6hw6-v9g4
> → GHSA-rwm7-x88c-3g2p
> → GHSA-6m6c-36f7-fhxh
> → GHSA-6cp8-v795-jr2j
> → GHSA-3wfc-mgpm-9rq6
> → GHSA-387m-935m-c4vw

🔗 [View commit](https://github.com/isaacs/node-tar/commit/9e78bf058b2c22dd4d52e00d8922d5c06fc2f7b5)

## GHSA-9hw9-ch79-4vh6 · pillow

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `BUFFER_OVERFLOW→HEAP`

### Root cause

The `ImageCmsTransform.apply()` method in Pillow did not validate that the input image's mode matched the transform's expected input mode before performing the color transformation. If `imOut` was not provided, a new image was created with `self.output_mode`, but the transformation was still applied using the original `im` (which could have a different mode), leading to a mismatch in buffer sizes during the underlying C function call.

### Impact

An attacker could provide a specially crafted image that, when processed, would cause a heap out-of-bounds write. This could lead to a denial of service (application crash) or potentially arbitrary code execution, depending on the overwritten memory region.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/PIL/ImageCms.py
+++ b/src/PIL/ImageCms.py
@@ -317,19 +317,21 @@ def point(self, im: Image.Image) -> Image.Image:
         return self.apply(im)
 
     def apply(self, im: Image.Image, imOut: Image.Image | None = None) -> Image.Image:
-        if imOut is None:
+        if im.mode != self.input_mode:
+            msg = "mode mismatch"
+            raise ValueError(msg)
+        if imOut is not None:
+            if imOut.mode != self.output_mode:
+                msg = "mode mismatch"
+                raise ValueError(msg)
+        else:
             imOut = Image.new(self.output_mode, im.size, None)
         self.transform.apply(im.getim(), imOut.getim())
         imOut.info["icc_profile"] = self.output_profile.tobytes()
         return imOut
 
     def apply_in_place(self, im: Image.Image) -> Image.Image:
-        if im.mode != self.output_mode:
-            msg = "mode mismatch"
-            raise ValueError(msg)  # wrong output mode
-        self.transform.apply(im.getim(), im.getim())
-        im.info["icc_profile"] = self.output_profile.tobytes()
-        return im
+        return self.apply(im, im)
```

### Fix summary

The patch adds explicit mode validation at the beginning of the `apply()` method. It now checks if `im.mode` matches `self.input_mode` and if `imOut.mode` (if provided) matches `self.output_mode`, raising a `ValueError` on mismatch. Additionally, `apply_in_place` now correctly calls `apply` with `im` as both input and output.

> ⚡ **Pattern match — 31 occurrences**
> Languages: Go, Ruby, Python, C#, JavaScript, RUST, Java
> → GHSA-378j-3jfj-8r9f
> → GHSA-6r34-94wq-jhrc
> → GHSA-q6vj-wxvf-5m8c

## GHSA-ffq3-xpv3-j92q · mistune

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `DOS→RESOURCE_EXHAUSTION`

### Root cause

The `parse_ref_link` function repeatedly searched for blank lines using `BLANK_LINE.search` within a loop for each reference link definition. When processing a large number of reference link definitions, especially if they were long, this repeated linear search led to quadratic time complexity, causing significant performance degradation.

### Impact

An attacker could craft a Markdown input with a large number of repeated reference link definitions, causing the parser to consume excessive CPU resources and time, leading to a denial of service for applications processing such input.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/mistune/block_parser.py
+++ b/src/mistune/block_parser.py
@@ -256,11 +257,11 @@ def parse_ref_link(self, m: Match[str], state: BlockState) -> Optional[int]:
 
         assert href_pos is not None
 
-        _blank = self.BLANK_LINE.search(state.src, href_pos)
-        if _blank:
-            max_pos = _blank.start()
-        else:
+        blank_pos = _find_next_blank_line(state, href_pos, self.BLANK_LINE)
+        if blank_pos is None:
             max_pos = state.cursor_max
+        else:
+            max_pos = blank_pos
```

### Fix summary

The patch introduces a new helper function `_find_next_blank_line` that pre-caches all blank line positions in the document using `finditer` once. Subsequent lookups for blank lines then use `bisect_left` on this cached list, reducing the complexity from linear search per link to logarithmic, thus preventing the quadratic time complexity.

## GHSA-jhmp-mqwm-3gq8 · tornado

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `UNCLASSIFIED`

## GHSA-jjj6-mw9f-p565 · Pillow

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `DOS→RESOURCE_EXHAUSTION`

### Root cause

The PdfParser.PdfStream.decode() method used zlib.decompress without imposing a limit on the output buffer size. An attacker could craft a PDF stream with a small compressed size but a very large decompressed size, leading to excessive memory consumption.

### Impact

An attacker could cause a denial of service by providing a specially crafted PDF file that, when processed, would consume an inordinate amount of memory, potentially crashing the application or the system.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/PIL/PdfParser.py
+++ b/src/PIL/PdfParser.py
@@ -320,17 +322,18 @@ def __init__(self, dictionary: PdfDict, buf: bytes) -> None:
         self.dictionary = dictionary
         self.buf = buf
 
-    def decode(self) -> bytes:
+    def decode(self, max_length: int = ImageFile.SAFEBLOCK) -> bytes:
         try:
             filter = self.dictionary[b"Filter"]
         except KeyError:
             return self.buf
         if filter == b"FlateDecode":
-            try:
-                expected_length = self.dictionary[b"DL"]
-            except KeyError:
-                expected_length = self.dictionary[b"Length"]
-            return zlib.decompress(self.buf, bufsize=int(expected_length))
+            dobj = zlib.decompressobj()
+            plaintext = dobj.decompress(self.buf, max_length)
+            if dobj.unconsumed_tail:
+                msg = "Decompressed data too large"
+                raise ValueError(msg)
+            return plaintext
```

### Fix summary

The patch introduces a 'max_length' parameter to the decode method, defaulting to ImageFile.SAFEBLOCK. It now uses zlib.decompressobj to decompress the data with this maximum length and checks if any unconsumed tail remains, raising a ValueError if the decompressed data exceeds the limit.

## GHSA-phj9-mv4w-65pm · pillow

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 7.5 · Pattern: `DOS→RESOURCE_EXHAUSTION`

### Root cause

The `GdImageFile._open()` method in Pillow processed image dimensions directly from the input file without performing a decompression bomb check. This allowed an attacker to craft a malicious GD image file with excessively large dimensions, which would consume an unreasonable amount of memory when processed.

### Impact

An attacker could cause a denial of service by providing a specially crafted GD image file, leading to excessive memory consumption and potential application crashes or system instability.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/PIL/GdImageFile.py
+++ b/src/PIL/GdImageFile.py
@@ -59,6 +59,7 @@ def _open(self) -> None:
 
         self._mode = "P"
         self._size = i16(s, 2), i16(s, 4)
+        Image._decompression_bomb_check(self.size)
 
         true_color = s[6]
```

### Fix summary

The patch adds a call to `Image._decompression_bomb_check(self.size)` immediately after reading the image dimensions. This ensures that the dimensions are validated against a maximum allowed size, preventing the allocation of excessively large image buffers.

> ⚡ **Pattern match — 91 occurrences**
> Languages: Go, Python, JavaScript, RUST, Java, C#, PHP, Ruby, Erlang
> → GHSA-h749-fxx7-pwpg
> → GHSA-hv3w-m4g2-5x77
> → GHSA-h9mw-h4qc-f5jf
> → GHSA-4g5x-2jfc-xm98
> → GHSA-whj4-6x5x-4v2j
> → GHSA-7gcj-phff-2884
> → GHSA-7c4j-2m43-2mgh

## GHSA-f4vv-55c2-5789 · lightrag-hku

2026-07-20 · Python · **CRITICAL** 0.0 · Pattern: `INSECURE_DEFAULT→CONFIG`

### Root cause

The vulnerability stemmed from an insecure default configuration where a hardcoded `DEFAULT_TOKEN_SECRET` was used to sign 'guest' tokens. When an API key was configured, the system would still accept these easily forgeable guest tokens, effectively bypassing the intended API key protection.

### Impact

An attacker could forge a 'guest' token using the publicly known default secret and use it to bypass API key authentication, gaining unauthorized access to protected API endpoints.

### Key diff

```
-                if not auth_configured and token_info.get("role") == "guest":
-                    return
-                # Accept non-guest token if auth is configured
-                if auth_configured and token_info.get("role") != "guest":
-                    return
+                if not auth_configured and token_info.get("role") == "guest":
+                    if not api_key_configured:
+                        return
+                elif auth_configured and token_info.get("role") != "guest":
+                    return
```

### Fix summary

The patch modifies the authentication logic to explicitly ignore 'guest' tokens when the system is configured for API-key-only authentication. This ensures that in API-key-only mode, the `X-API-Key` header remains the sole authority for authentication, preventing guest tokens from bypassing it.

> ⚡ **Pattern match — 19 occurrences**

## GHSA-gcfj-64vw-6mp9 · axios

2026-07-20 · JavaScript · **HIGH** 0.0 · Pattern: `PROTOTYPE_POLLUTION→OVERRIDE`

### Root cause

The Axios HTTP adapter, when processing configuration objects, did not properly check for the existence of properties directly on the `config` object. Instead, it accessed properties like `config.auth`, `config.proxy`, and `config.paramsSerializer` directly. This allowed an attacker to inject malicious properties into the `Object.prototype` (prototype pollution), which would then be inherited and used by Axios during request processing, even if the `config` object itself didn't explicitly define them.

### Impact

An attacker could potentially control proxy settings, authentication credentials, or URL serialization logic by polluting the prototype chain. This could lead to information disclosure (e.g., proxying requests to an attacker-controlled server), unauthorized access, or other unexpected behavior during HTTP requests.

### Key diff

```
--- a/lib/adapters/http.js
+++ b/lib/adapters/http.js
@@ -373,9 +373,10 @@ module.exports = function httpAdapter(config) {
 
     // HTTP basic authentication
     var auth = undefined;
-    if (config.auth) {
-      var username = config.auth.username || '';
-      var password = config.auth.password || '';
+    var configAuth = utils.hasOwnProperty(config, 'auth') ? config.auth : undefined;
+    if (configAuth) {
+      var username = utils.hasOwnProperty(configAuth, 'username') ? configAuth.username || '' : '';
+      var password = utils.hasOwnProperty(configAuth, 'password') ? configAuth.password || '' : '';
       auth = username + ':' + password;
     }
```

### Fix summary

The patch introduces `utils.hasOwnProperty(config, 'propertyName')` checks before accessing potentially sensitive properties like `auth`, `proxy`, and `paramsSerializer` from the `config` object. This ensures that Axios only uses properties explicitly defined on the `config` object itself, preventing inherited properties from the prototype chain from being used.

> ⚡ **Pattern match — 14 occurrences**
> Languages: JavaScript
> → GHSA-fw9q-39r9-c252
> → GHSA-rv5g-f82m-qrvv
> → GHSA-mhwj-73qx-jqxm
> → GHSA-xp7r-j8r6-j9h3
> → GHSA-m2hg-wjq3-28wq
> → GHSA-cmxg-94mg-jq94
> → GHSA-c4cf-2hgv-2qv6
> → GHSA-q3fm-4wcw-g57x
> → GHSA-hp36-v28f-w3r4
> → GHSA-9m6g-wc8r-q59c
> → GHSA-2933-q333-qg83

## GHSA-vjc4-5qp5-m44j · pillow

2026-07-20 · Python · **HIGH** 0.0 · Pattern: `DOS→RESOURCE_EXHAUSTION`

### Root cause

The `total_component_width` variable was initialized once outside the tile processing loop. When processing tiled JPEG2000 images, this variable was not reset for each new tile, leading to a continuously growing scratch buffer as more tiles were decoded. A malicious file could exploit this by crafting an image with many tiles.

### Impact

An attacker could provide a specially crafted JPEG2000 image to a system using Pillow, causing it to consume an excessive amount of memory and potentially crash the application or the system, leading to a denial of service.

### Key diff

```
--- a/src/libImaging/Jpeg2KDecode.c
+++ b/src/libImaging/Jpeg2KDecode.c
@@ -645,7 +645,6 @@ j2k_decode_entry(Imaging im, ImagingCodecState state) {
     size_t tile_bytes = 0;
     unsigned n, tile_height, tile_width;
     int subsampling;
-    int total_component_width = 0;
 
     stream = opj_stream_create(BUFFER_SIZE, OPJ_TRUE);
 
@@ -851,6 +850,7 @@ j2k_decode_entry(Imaging im, ImagingCodecState state) {
          a, and then a malicious file could have a smaller tile_bytes
         */
 
+        int total_component_width = 0;
         for (n = 0; n < tile_info.nb_comps; n++) {
             // see csize /acsize calcs
             int csize = (image->comps[n].prec + 7) >> 3;
```

### Fix summary

The patch moves the initialization of `total_component_width` inside the tile processing loop. This ensures that the variable is reset for each new tile, preventing the scratch buffer from growing indefinitely and mitigating the memory exhaustion vulnerability.

> ⚡ **Pattern match — 91 occurrences**
